    [Original] AI chatbots pose 'dangerous' risk when giving medical advice, study suggests
    ShareSaveLaura CressTechnology reporterShareSaveFiordaliso via Getty ImagesAI chatbots give inaccurate and inconsistent medical advice that could present risks to users, according to a study from the University of Oxford. The research found people using AI for healthcare advice were given a mix of good and bad responses, making it hard to identify what advice they should trust. In November 2025, polling by Mental Health UK found more than one in three UK residents now use AI to support their mental health or wellbeing. Dr Rebecca Payne, lead medical practitioner on the study, said it could be "dangerous" for people to ask chatbots about their symptoms. Researchers gave 1,300 people a scenario, such as having a severe headache or being a new mother who felt constantly exhausted. They were split into two groups, with one using AI to help them figure out what they might have and decide what to do next. The researchers then evaluated whether people correctly identified what might be wrong, and if they should see a GP or go to A&E. They said the people who used AI often did not know what to ask, and were given a variety of different answers depending on how they worded their question. The chatbot responded with a mixture of information, and people found it hard to distinguish between what was useful and what was not. Dr Adam Mahdi, senior author on the study, told the BBC while AI was able to give medical information, people "struggle to get useful advice from it". "People share information gradually", he said. "They leave things out, they don't mention everything. So, in our study, when the AI listed three possible conditions, people were left to guess which of those can fit. "This is exactly when things would fall apart." Lead author Andrew Bean said the analysis illustrated how interacting with humans poses a challenge "even for top" AI models. "We hope this work will contribute to the development of safer and more useful AI systems," he said. Dr Amber W. Childs, an associate professor of psychiatry in the Yale School of Medicine, said since chatbots are trained on current medical practices and data, they also face a further problem of repeating biases which have been "baked into medical practices for decades". "A chatbot is only as good a diagnostician as seasoned clinicians are, which is not perfect either," she said. Meanwhile Dr Bertalan MeskÃ³, editor of The Medical Futurist, which predicts tech trends in healthcare, said there were developments coming in the space. He said two major AI developers, OpenAI and Anthropic, had released health-dedicated versions of their general chatbot recently, which he believed would "definitely yield different results in a similar study". He said the goal should be to "to keep on improving" the tech, especially "health-related versions, with clear national regulations, regulatory guardrails and medical guidelines".     [Original] EU tells Meta to let rivals run AI chatbots on WhatsApp
    ShareSaveLaura CressTechnology reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesThe EU has told Meta it has breached its rules by blocking other rival AI firms' chatbots from WhatsApp, and must make "urgent" changes. The tech giant changed the popular messaging app on 15 January - and since then only its AI assistant Meta AI can access it. But the European Commission said WhatsApp was an "important entry point" for AI chatbots like ChatGPT to reach people, and claimed Meta was abusing its dominant position by blocking them. A Meta spokesperson told the BBC the EU had "no reason" to intervene, and claimed it had "incorrectly" assumed WhatsApp Business was a key way that people use chatbots. "We must protect effective competition in this vibrant field, which means we cannot allow dominant tech companies to illegally leverage their dominance to give themselves an unfair advantage," said Teresa Ribera, the European Commission's competition chief. The EU will wait for Meta to formally respond to its findings, and depending on the response, it could impose "interim measures" to prevent Meta from causing "serious and irreparable harm on the market". Mathias Vermeulen, director at AWO, a law firm working on EU digital policies, said the preliminary findings showed firms operating in the EU could not use their control over one market "to unfairly advantage themselves in another". He said while Meta had not been found to break the law yet, if interim measures were imposed, Meta may be forced to reopen WhatsApp to third-party AI assistants. It follows a push by the EU to investigate big tech firms through its digital laws. It comes just three days after the Commission told TikTok it must change its "addictive design" or face heavy fines, after it found the video sharing platform had breached its online safety rules. It has also launched an inquiry in January into Elon Musk's X over concerns its AI tool Grok was used to create sexualised images of real people.     [Original] Meta and YouTube Created â€˜Digital Casinos,â€™ Lawyers Argue in Landmark Trial
    In a Los Angeles courtroom, opening statements began in a landmark trial accusing Meta (owner of Instagram) and YouTube (owned by Google) of designing addictive social media platforms that caused personal injury to a 20-year-old California woman, identified as K.G.M. Lawyer Mark Lanier opened with a demonstration using three wooden childrenâ€™s blocks, declaring, â€œAddicting, brains, children,â€ and arguing that the companies built â€œtrapsâ€ to create lifelong users. K.G.M., who created a YouTube account at age 8 and joined Instagram at 9, alleges her use of the platforms led to anxiety, depression, and body dysmorphia due to beauty filters. Her case, one of nine bundled in a California Superior Court trial, is among the strongest personal injury claims in a wave of lawsuits accusing tech giants of prioritizing profit over youth safety. The trial, presided over by Judge Carolyn B. Kuhl and decided by a jury, tests a novel legal theory likening social media to cigarettes and casinos. Plaintiffs argue that features like infinite scroll, auto-play, and algorithmic recommendations drive compulsive use, citing internal company documents. One 2018 Meta document stated, â€œIf we want to win big with teens, we must bring them in as tweens,â€ noting users joining at 11 had four times the long-term retention. Another document compared Metaâ€™s tactics to those of tobacco companies. YouTubeâ€™s 2011 presentation even pitched the platform as a babysitter for children under 4. While Snap and TikTok settled with K.G.M. under undisclosed terms, they remain defendants in other cases. The trial, expected to last six to eight weeks, may feature testimony from Meta CEO Mark Zuckerberg and YouTube CEO Neal Mohan. Meanwhile, a separate trial in New Mexico began Monday, where New Mexicoâ€™s Attorney General RaÃºl Torrez accused Meta of enabling child sexual exploitation and deploying harmful chatbots. State attorneys general across the U.S. have filed dozens of similar suits. Internationally, Australia banned children under 16 from social media, while the EU, UK, and others have enacted child safety regulations. In Oakland, federal cases involving school districts and states are set for trial this summer, arguing social media constitutes a public nuisance. During a lunch break, parents of children harmed by social media, including Lori Schott whose daughter died by suicide in 2020 after viewing self-harm content, embraced in tears, saying, â€œWe are here to learn the truth.â€ Lawyers for Meta and YouTube are scheduled to deliver their opening statements later Monday.

    [Original] A.I. Is Making Doctors Answer a Question: What Are They Really Good For?
    Many physicians, including Stanford internist Dr. Jonathan Chen, are grappling with the existential threat posed by artificial intelligence, which excels at diagnosis, image analysis, patient communication, and administrative tasksâ€”areas once central to doctorsâ€™ roles. While some, like Yale cardiologist Dr. Harlan Krumholz, acknowledge A.I.â€™s superior diagnostic reasoning, others fear their professional identity is at risk. Dr. Robert Califf of Duke notes that A.I. is already handling â€œscutworkâ€ like clinical documentation, yet cannot replicate the nuanced judgment required in complex cases. Dr. Lee Schwamm of Yale emphasizes that while A.I. excels at pattern recognition and prediction, it lacks the ability to extract meaning from ambiguous patient descriptionsâ€”such as distinguishing between lightheadedness and vertigo, or numbness versus weaknessâ€”skills honed through years of training and clinical experience. Human connection remains vital, especially when delivering difficult news. Despite these concerns, A.I. is already transforming care delivery. Dr. John Erik Pandolfino at Northwestern developed GERDBot, an A.I. triage tool that redirects less urgent GERD cases to nurse practitioners or physician assistants, reducing wait times from months to days. His newer algorithm, Eso-Instein, aims to guide less specialized gastroenterologists in diagnosing and treating esophageal conditions, potentially making specialists like him less essential over time. â€œEventually, when the algorithm is working better than a human, I will have to find something different to do,â€ he said. A.I. also holds promise in addressing the shortage of primary care providers, especially in urban and rural areas. Dr. Adam Rodman of Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center sees A.I. as a way to triage patients and expand the reach of nurse practitioners, allowing physicians to focus on complex cases. However, experts like Dr. Leo Anthony Celi and Marzyeh Ghassemi of MIT warn that A.I. risks amplifying existing biasesâ€”such as underestimating women or patients with poor grammarâ€”and is often used to boost profits rather than reform a flawed system. Still, Rodman believes A.I. can help identify and reduce bias more effectively than humans. While some tasksâ€”like adhering to screening guidelines or counseling on sleep and dietâ€”may be better handled by A.I., doctors remain essential for integrating complex, imperfect information and delivering compassionate care.

    [Original] These A.I. Dreamers Donâ€™t Fit the Stereotype
    At 24, Marshall Kools, a co-founder of the AI start-up Arzana, lives in a NoPa San Francisco apartment with two roommates, sleeps in a nook beneath the stairs, and earns about $10,000 annually.     A landmark trial in Los Angeles Superior Court has begun, accusing Meta (parent company of Instagram) and YouTube (owned by Google) of creating â€œaddiction machinesâ€ that harm childrenâ€™s mental health. In his opening argument, plaintiffâ€™s attorney Mark Lanier claimed that his client, referred to as K.G.M. (or Kaley G.M.), developed severe mental health issues due to social media addiction engineered by the platforms. â€œThese companies built machines designed to addict the brains of children, and they did it on purpose,â€ Lanier asserted, emphasizing that the companies failed to warn users of the risks. He used a visual display of childrenâ€™s blocks labeled â€œAddicting,â€ â€œBrains,â€ and â€œChildrenâ€ to underscore his argument, stating the case involves â€œtwo of the richest corporations in history who have engineered addiction in childrenâ€™s brains.â€ Lanier cited internal documents and a 2015 email from Meta CEO Mark Zuckerberg demanding a 12% increase in user time spent on platforms, highlighting deliberate design choices to maximize engagement.     [Original] Businesses face extinction unless they protect nature, major report warns
    ShareSaveHelen Briggs,Environment correspondentandGwyndaf Hughes,Science videographerShareSaveWWTExperts are calling for urgent action to restore the natural systems that keep the economy runningBusinesses risk extinction themselves unless they protect and restore the natural world, scientists across the globe are warning. A major new report sets out how companies can shift from harmful approaches to ones that help restore nature. It comes amid major concerns over UK nature loss. The assessment, by Ipbes (Intergovernmental Science-Policy Platform on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services), is based on contributions from leading scientists and approved by 150 governments. Businesses can either lead the way or "ultimately risk extinction... both of species in nature, but potentially also their own", said co-author Matt Jones, from the UN World Conservation Monitoring Centre in Cambridge. Ipbes found that all businesses, even those seemingly far-removed from it, rely on the services nature provides for free, from clean water to fertile soils. Co-chair, Prof Stephen Polasky, said the loss of biodiversity is among the most serious threats to business, "yet the twisted reality is that it often seems more profitable to businesses to degrade biodiversity than to protect it". Commenting on the report, Leigh Morris, International Director of the consortium of UK wildlife charities, The Wildlife Trusts, said we need "clear metrics and toolkits so businesses can get their own houses in order on biodiversity". For many UK businesses, engaging with nature protection has gone from "a nice-to-do" to a "must-do", he added. One example of how companies are working hand-in-hand with nature is at Steart Marshes in Somerset. Farmers and conservationists are managing land in ways that support both wildlife and agriculture. Cattle are grazed across the marshes to create healthy habitats for birds, insects and plants. Local farmer, Andy Darch, said the animals are helping in conservation. "They are eating different types of grasses and trampling areas that are important for wildlife," he said. "And because they are eating such a variety of plants, it produces a really high-quality beef at the end of the day." Steart Marshes also demonstrates how restoring nature can benefit local communities. Newly engineered flood banks protect nearby villages from rising waters, while the wetlands help absorb carbon and reduce pollution. Alys Laver, from the Wildfowl and Wetlands Trust, which runs the nature reserve, said farming and conservation are working hand-in-hand. "Not only are those flood banks an engineering marvel, they're providing flood protection for the local villages," she says. "On the back of that we're getting a product the farmers can utilise. So it's not a loss of farming it's just a change." The report pointed to everyday incentives that are driving nature's decline: It said better stewardship of nature is not a distant environmental issue but a core challenge for every boardroom. Yet, less than 1% of publicly reporting companies mention their impacts on biodiversity in their reports.

    One of Britainâ€™s last family-run make-up brands, Barry M, has been acquired out of administration by cosmetics rival Warpaint in a Â£1.4 million deal. Founded in north London in 1982 by Barry Mero, the brand collapsed earlier this month after struggling to innovate amid rising competition. Since Barry Meroâ€™s death in 2014, the company has been led by his son, Dean Mero, who recalled his fatherâ€™s vision to offer 100 coloursâ€”making Barry M the first brand to achieve that milestone. Known for its bold, vibrant shades popular with make-up enthusiasts and drag queens, Barry M was stocked in around 1,300 UK retailers, including Superdrug, Boots, Sainsburyâ€™s, and Tesco.     [Original] The tech firms embracing a 72-hour working week
    Rilla, a New York-based tech company developing AI-driven sales monitoring systems, has drawn attention for its recruitment website promoting a 996 work cultureâ€”9 a.m. to 9 p.m., six days a weekâ€”advertised as a badge of honor. He emphasizes flexibility, noting that employees may work late into the night and return midday, reflecting a culture where long hours are voluntary and intrinsic to innovation. This â€œhustle cultureâ€ has gained traction in the U.S. tech sector, particularly among AI startups racing to monetize rapid advancements. Adrian Kinnersley, a recruitment expert, attributes the trend to venture capital pressure: companies fear being outpaced, making extended hours seem essential. Magnus MÃ¼ller, co-founder of AI startup Browser-Use, exemplifies this mindset, living in a â€œhacker-houseâ€ where constant collaboration is the norm. He views long hours as necessary for deep problem immersion, calling it â€œlike gamingâ€â€”a passion, not work. He explicitly rejects candidates seeking a 40-hour week. However, critics argue the model is unsustainable. He cautions that such cultures alienate family-oriented employees and risk burnout. Tamara Myles, a workplace culture expert, highlights power imbalancesâ€”employees may stay due to economic pressure or visa dependence, not genuine choice. Health risks are well-documented. The WHO and ILO reported in 2021 that working 55+ hours weekly led to 745,000 deaths globally from stroke and heart disease in 2016, increasing stroke risk by 35% and heart disease by 17%. Japanâ€™s term â€œkarÅshiâ€ (death from overwork) reflects long-standing concerns. Research confirms productivity peaks around 40 hours, declining sharply beyond that threshold.     [Original] The shadowy world of abandoned oil tankers
    The number of abandoned oil tankers and commercial ships has surged globally, rising from 20 in 2016 to 410 in 2025, affecting 6,223 merchant seamenâ€”up nearly a third from 2024. The International Transport Workersâ€™ Federation (ITF) reports that 82% of abandoned vessels in 2025 were flagged under flags of convenience (FOCs), with shadow fleetsâ€”aging, unseaworthy ships often unregistered or under obscure ownershipâ€”playing a major role. These vessels, frequently used to circumvent Western sanctions on Russia, Iran, and Venezuela, sail under FOCs like Panama, Liberia, and the Marshall Islands, which collectively account for 46.5% of global merchant tonnage. The Gambia, with no oil tankers registered in 2023, hosted 35 by March 2025, earning substantial fees. Ivan, a Russian senior deck officer, spoke from an abandoned oil tanker carrying nearly 750,000 barrels of Russian crudeâ€”valued at $50 millionâ€”sailing from Russiaâ€™s Far East to China. Abandoned in December 2024 after months without pay, the crew faced severe shortages of food, water, and medical supplies, impacting their health and morale. The ship remains in international waters, with China unwilling to allow entry due to scrutiny. The ITF intervened, securing payment up to December and delivering essential supplies. While some crew have been repatriated, most, including Ivan, remain on board. Abandonment is defined by the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) as failure to cover repatriation, provide maintenance, or pay wages for at least two months. In 2025, seafarers were owed $25.8 million globally; the ITF recovered $16.5 million. Indian sailors were most affected (1,125 cases), followed by Filipinos (539) and Syrians (309). India blacklisted 86 foreign vessels in September 2024 over abandonment and rights violations, many with untraceable owners or no flag state response. Mark Dickinson of Nautilus International criticized FOC states for failing to uphold responsibility, calling for a genuine link between shipowners and their flagsâ€”mandated under international law but lacking universal definition. Ivanâ€™s vessel was flying a false Gambian flag; The Gambia has since opened an inquiry. ITF inspector Nathan Smith expects resolution only after a ship-to-ship transfer of oil in open sea. Ivan now vows to research vessel safety and sanctions status before joining future voyages. With shadow fleets central to sanctioned oil trade, experts stress the need for stronger international cooperation to protect seafarers.

    [Original] Plane makers chase Asia's super-rich with luxe new private jets
    At the Singapore Airshow, Gulfstreamâ€™s G700 private jet drew long queues of visitors eager to tour its spacious, light-filled cabin featuring a â€œgrand suite with a showerâ€ and living areas with sofas and TV consoles. The aircraft exemplifies a growing trend: rising demand for private jets, particularly in the Asia-Pacific region. In 2025, global private jet flights reached 3.7 millionâ€”up 5% from 2024 and 35% higher than pre-pandemic levels, according to WingX. The number of ultra-high-net-worth individuals (worth over $30 million) grew by more than 70% between 2020 and 2025. Scott Neal, Gulfstreamâ€™s head of worldwide sales, cited increased global business travel as a key driver, noting that private jets offer efficiency over commercial flights with fewer stopovers. Rivals including Dassault, Bombardier, Embraer, and Textron Aviation are also competing in the market. Dassault highlights its Falcon jets, leveraging military-grade technology for performance and comfort, with customers including corporations, governments, and high-net-worth individuals. Carlos Brana, Dassaultâ€™s head of civil aircraft, emphasized efficiency over extravagance: â€œI want to go from that point to that point as fast as I can and not lose time in stopovers.â€ Manufacturers focus on reducing fatigue through improved cabin pressure, noise reduction, and refined interiors. Asia-Pacific is a key growth region: international traffic grew 8% in 2025, outpacing the global average of 6.8%. Gulfstream reports strong demand in Vietnam, Singapore, Indonesia, Malaysia, Australia, and New Zealand, while Dassault notes rising interest in India, Thailand, and Laosâ€”especially where shorter runways limit commercial airliner access. China, once the regionâ€™s largest private jet market, has cooled, but demand is expected to rebound as Chinese firms expand globally. Despite Asia-Pacificâ€™s smaller share of the global business aviation fleet compared to the U.S. (which accounts for about 70%), growth remains robust. Gulfstream says its newest jets can run on 100% sustainable aviation fuel (SAF), while Dassault supports a 50-50 blend. However, SAF production remains limited and costly. Manufacturers argue newer models are 35% more fuel-efficient than previous generations and reduce emissions through longer-range flights.     [Original] Pilots and Flight Attendants Criticize American Airlinesâ€™ Performance
    Pilots and flight attendants at American Airlines have publicly expressed deep dissatisfaction with company leadership, marking a rare escalation in internal tensions. The Association of Professional Flight Attendants, representing 28,000 flight attendants, issued a unanimous vote of no confidence in CEO Robert Isom, calling it the first formal such action in the unionâ€™s history. The Allied Pilots Association, representing 16,000 pilots, followed with a request to meet the board, citing that American is â€œon an underperforming pathâ€ and lacks a clear strategy to improve. Notably, neither union is currently in contract negotiations, underscoring the severity of the discontent. American has fallen significantly behind Delta Air Lines and United Airlines in profitability. According to S&P Capital IQ, Delta captured about 56% of U.S. airline industry profits in 2024 and 2025, United 43%, and American just 6%, despite being the largest carrier by flight and passenger volume. Pilots and flight attendants at all three airlines earn profit-sharing, making performance a direct concern.     [Original] Court TV, Known for Live Trial Coverage, Is Getting a New Owner
    Court TV, which brings viewers into courtrooms for everything from murder trials to defamation cases, has been a staple of cable television for decades. Now, itâ€™s getting a new owner that wants to go bigger on streaming. On Monday, E.W. Scripps announced that it had reached a deal to sell the channel to the parent company of Law&Crime. That network, which was created by the ABC legal analyst Dan Abrams, has more than eight million YouTube subscribers and offers programming that includes live trials, commentary and crime documentaries. Court TV will maintain its programming on basic cable and has signed a three-year distribution deal with Scripps. But some content may be developed for YouTube or social media platforms, Mr. Abrams said in an interview. The deal values Court TV at less than $125 million, according to two people familiar with the talks, who requested anonymity because the figure is confidential. â€œThe goal is going to be to try to transform what is a legacy media company into a YouTube and digital-media-first business,â€ Mr. Abrams said. Law&Crime will retain about a dozen of Court TVâ€™s employees, Mr. Abrams said. Court TV currently has more than 50 employees, according to one of the people familiar with the talks. Scripps owns 60 stations in more than 40 markets, including ION, a general entertainment and sports network, and Bounce, which describes itself as the first African American broadcast network. Its shares are down about 95 percent over the past year as more customers cut the cable cord in favor of streaming and as it grapples with debt of about $2.7 billion. Scripps has also been fending off a takeover attempt by Sinclair, a rival broadcaster. Scripps acquired the rights to Court TV from Turner Broadcasting in 2018, in an attempt to retain cable viewers. It has since covered high-profile trials including the defamation case that the actor Johnny Depp brought against Amber Heard and her countersuit, as well as the murder trial for Alex Murdaugh. Adam Symson, the Scripps president and chief executive, said in a statement that the sale was â€œconsistentâ€ with the companyâ€™s business strategy of making moves that â€œstrengthen our balance sheet and position us for the future.â€ Mr. Abrams, who rose to fame covering the O.J. Simpson trial for Court TV, founded Law&Crime in 2015. (The studio sued Court TV in 2021 for advertising itself as the only destination to watch live court coverage.) Mr. Abrams sold Law&Crime to the media company Jellysmack in 2023 for around $125 million. He remains the brandâ€™s chief executive. Law&Crime will focus more on true-crime dramas and Court TV will focus more on trials, Mr. Abrams said. â€œFifteen, 20 years ago, it was legacy media companies that would acquire a digital first,â€ Mr. Abrams said. â€œAnd here we are as a YouTube-first and digital-first true-crime company acquiring a legacy media.â€ Lauren Hirsch is a Times reporter who covers deals and dealmakers in Wall Street and Washington.     [Original] Hollywood Braces for New Round of Labor Talks
    Just 26 months after the historic 2023 Hollywood strikes ended, negotiations for new contracts are set to begin on Monday between SAG-AFTRA, representing actors, and the Alliance of Motion Picture and Television Producers, the studiosâ€™ bargaining group. The current agreements expire May 1 for writers and June 30 for actors. The industry has since transformed: production has slowed amid the shift to streaming, work has dwindled for creatives, and generative artificial intelligence has emerged as a central issue. SAG-AFTRA chief negotiator Duncan Crabtree-Ireland emphasized the unionâ€™s commitment to securing fair deals, stating, â€œWe are not going to accept a deal thatâ€™s unfair to our membership.â€ The studio alliance expressed optimism about reaching a fair agreement that supports performers and ensures long-term stability. The Writers Guild of America will begin talks in March, followed by the Directors Guild of America later in the spring. The 2023 strikesâ€”unprecedented since the 1960sâ€”halted production for months and caused significant economic disruption. Key issues then included compensation, working conditions, and AI use, all of which remain central. Crabtree-Ireland said the union will push for stronger guardrails on AI. SAG-AFTRA President Sean Astin, who replaced Fran Drescher last year, highlighted streaming residuals and other â€œmeat and potatoesâ€ issues as priorities, noting that current contracts no longer reflect membersâ€™ lived experiences. The Writers Guild has not yet detailed its top demands, but replenishing health care reserves is expected to be a major focus. Board member Marguerite MacIntyre said the health care funding question would be â€œa headline issue,â€ echoed by Michele Mulroney, president of the WGAâ€™s western branch. Studios are reportedly seeking five-year contractsâ€”two years longer than the typical three-year dealsâ€”citing stability models from the NFL (10-year) and longshoremenâ€™s union (six-year). Union leaders argue that shorter contracts provide necessary flexibility amid rapid technological change. Christopher Nolan, newly elected DGA president, dismissed five-year deals as â€œnot realistic.â€ Greg Hessinger, a new studio negotiator, replaces the previous lead. Crabtree-Ireland, who knew Hessinger from decades ago, said he expects a â€œlikely vibe shiftâ€ and expressed cautious optimism about improved industry relations. John Koblin covers television for The New York Times.

    [Original] Extreme cold in New York City leaves 18 dead
    ShareSaveAmy WalkerShareSaveGetty ImagesTemperatures are rising after days of extreme cold and snow in the city A total of 18 people have died as a result of a period of extreme cold weather in New York City, its mayor has said. Since late January, the city has endured a cold snap, including 13 days of temperatures of 0C (32F) or below â€“ one of the longest stretches of sub-zero weather New York has seen in six decades. Over the weekend, another person "lost their life on the streets of our city," Zohran Mamdani said on Monday, adding that "each life lost is a tragedy, and we will continue to hold their families in our thoughts". While temperatures are set to rise this week, they remain below average, with Mamdani telling residents to "stay safe, stay indoors... [and] keep looking out for one another". The mayor added that since 19 January, when a Code Blue emergency was announced - which relaxed intake policies for homeless shelters - about 1,400 placements had been made into shelters. An additional 64 hotel rooms had been added to the city's shelter capacity, with at least another 150 outreach workers on the streets, said Mamdani. On 27 January, Mamdani said at least 10 of the people who had died were found outdoors. The circumstances of the other deaths are not known. "We have been working hard to keep New Yorkers safe, and we will continue to do so. Because it is not forecast to be above 32 degrees [Fahrenheit] until tomorrow, and 35 degrees is hardly balmy weather," he said. The US National Weather Service (NWS) said temperatures would increase to about 0C for the most part of the week, following persistent freezing temperatures on Monday. It added that highs were normally around 4C at this time in the year. An Arctic airmass brought hazardous sub-zero "wind chills" - the temperature it feels like rather than the temperature it is - to the region over the weekend, bringing a risk of hypothermia and frostbite, the forecaster said. A 13-day streak of temperatures being at 0C or below -one of the longest streaks for New York City since 1963 - ended on Friday after temperatures briefly tipped above 0C. NYC's Emergency Management agency said that following days of extreme cold and snow pose serious safety risks remained across the city. It warned that melting snow and ice could fall from buildings without warning and that streets and pavements could refreeze overnight.

    [Original] Norway police investigate diplomat over Epstein links
    ShareSaveTom McArthurShareSaveLev Radin/Pacific Press/LightRocket via Getty ImagesPolice in Norway have opened an investigation into a high-profile diplomat and her husband over their links to the late sex offender Jeffrey Epstein. Mona Juul resigned as ambassador to Jordan and Iraq on Sunday, days after she was suspended following Norwegian media reports that each of her children would receive $5m (Â£3.6m) in a will allegedly signed by Epstein days before he died. Emails released in the so-called Epstein files also appear to show that Juul's husband, Terje RÃ¸d-Larsen, had dinner with Epstein in Paris in June 2019, weeks before Epstein's arrest in the US on sex trafficking charges. Juul's lawyer has said she "does not recognise the accusations made against her" and RÃ¸d-Larsen's lawyer said he is confident the investigation will clarify "there is no basis for criminal liability". Appearing in the files is not an indication of wrongdoing. Norway's foreign ministry has said Juul's contact with Epstein revealed a "serious lapse in judgement". An "aggravated corruption" inquiry has been launched by the Norwegian economic crime unit Ã˜kokrim, police announced on Monday. "Ã˜kokrim will, among other things, investigate whether benefits were received in connection with her position," police said in a statement, referring to Juul. A property in Oslo has been searched in connection with the investigation. Juul and RÃ¸d-Larsen were part of a small group of diplomats that negotiated the 1993-1995 Oslo Accords, which at the time were seen as a breakthrough in the conflict between Israel and the Palestinians. Norwegian media reports RÃ¸d-Larsen had extensive communication with Epstein and arranged to meet him with Juul more than once. The wider Epstein documents, released by the US Department of Justice last month, contain millions of private emails, images and FBI reports into the late sex offender - who died in 2019 while awaiting trial for sex trafficking. Juul is the latest prominent public figure from the Nordic country to be drawn into the widening scandal surrounding the contents of the files, including Crown Princess Mette-Marit, former Prime Minister ThorbjÃ¸rn Jagland, and chief executive of the World Economic Forum (WEF), Borge Brende. Mette-Marit gave a "profound apology" to Norwegians for her friendship with Epstein, after it emerged they had exchanged messages for three years. Jagland, who is also former head of the Norwegian Nobel Committee, is being investigated over his alleged links to Epstein. His lawyer said they would fully co-operate with the police probe. The WEF ordered an independent review into Brende â€“ who also served as the Norwegian foreign minister - over his interactions with the paedophile. Brende has acknowledged dining with Epstein three times in 2018 and 2019 and communicating with him by email and text. He said he welcomes the WEF's decision to launch a review.

    [Original] Machine guns to machetes: Weapons that massacred thousands in Iran
    A BBC News Persian analysis of hundreds of verified videos and photographs from protests across more than 200 Iranian cities confirms the widespread use of lethal weaponry by security forces, including machine guns, sniper rifles, shotguns, and 20mm automatic cannon rounds. The crackdown, initially sparked by economic grievances, has escalated into what experts describe as the largest mass killing in contemporary Iranian history. Payam Akhavan, an Iranian-Canadian former UN prosecutor, called it â€œone of the largest in the world.â€ While the Iranian government blames â€œrioters and terroristsâ€ for the violence, evidence shows systematic use of heavy arms. Footage from Tehran, Isfahan, Yazdanshahr, and Shahsavar reveals DShK machine gunsâ€”identified by weapons expert Amael Kotlarski as the DShKM modelâ€”mounted on military pickup trucks, with similar vehicles seen in a 2025 IRGC parade. A video from Yazdanshahr shows sustained machine-gun fire, while another from Behesht Square in Tehran displays spent AK-47 casings and a large 20mm cannon round, confirmed by Thames Valley Guns as designed for military hardware, not personnelâ€”though its impact on humans is catastrophic. Sniper activity is documented in Mashhad, with a Dragunov SVD rifle seen on a rooftop, and a forensic expert confirmed sniper kills on Iranian state TV. The body of Ebrahim Pourahmadian, shot in the forehead in Tehran, shows a precise wound consistent with a sniper. Witnesses report psychological terror from unseen snipers. AK-47s and Iranian-made KL variants are widely used, with 7.62x39mm casings recovered and confirmed by Kotlarski. Shotguns, including Iranian-made Maher models, were deployed in Lordegan and Abadan, leading to mass eye injuries. Dr Ghasem Fakhraei reported 1,000 patients with ruptured eyeballs requiring emergency surgery in one hospital alone between 9â€“10 January. CT scans from Mashhad confirm metal pellets embedded in eye tissue. Plainclothes agents in Kermanshah and Tehran are seen wielding Beretta 50 or CZ 75 pistols, while videos show machetes used to attack protesters seeking refuge in buildings. A female protester was struck in the head with a machete in Tehran, and CCTV confirms such attacks. Survivors report beatings with batons and gun butts; Saghar Seifollahi Fars was killed this way, and Ali Taherkhani died after being shot and then beaten. The evidence collectively indicates a coordinated, high-level use of lethal force against unarmed civilians.
